The Interpretation of Allah's Laughter
Sanusi interprets the hadith of Allah's laughter as a metaphor for His pleasure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi interprets the hadith of Allah's laughter as a metaphor for divine pleasure, using Arabic linguistic and poetic evidence.

Core Idea
Allah's laughter is a metaphor for His pleasure, not a literal attribute.

This passage addresses a well-known hadith that ascribes laughter to Allah. Sanusi, following the path of metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil), provides linguistic evidence from Arabic usage and poetry to show that laughter can mean manifestation of pleasure. Before reading, consider how the same method applies to other divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the poetic line about 'showing molars'.
2
Identify the linguistic examples
The passage gives three examples: the palm tree laugh, gardens laugh, and showing molars. Each illustrates how laughter can mean manifestation. Understand each one.
These are common Arabic idioms for emphasis.
3
Understand the poetic evidence
The poet uses 'showing molars' to exaggerate evil's appearance. The author uses this to show that such phrases are not literal. Reflect on how poetry supports the interpretation.
The poet is from the pre-Islamic era.
4
Grasp the final interpretation
The author concludes that the hadith means Allah manifests His pleasure and reward to His friends. This is the intended meaning, not a literal laugh.
This aligns with divine transcendence (tanzih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما روي أنه تعالى يضحك إلى أوليائه أي ظهر الرضا عنهم يقال: ضحكت الطلعة إذا أظهرت ما في جوفها. ويقال: ضحكت الرياض بأنوارها إذا ظهرت، ويقولون إذا أرادوا المبالغة في وصف ظهور الشيء: أبدى ناجذيه. قال القائل: قوم إذا الشر أبدى ناجذيه لهم طاروا إليه ذرافات ووحدانا وليس للشر ناجذان في الحقيقة، وإنما المراد المبالغة في ظهوره تشبيها بمن يبالغ في ضحكه أنه يظهر ناجذيه، وإذا بالغ في ذلك فكان تأويل الخبر: إظهار الله تعالى رضاه وثوابه على أوليائه.
And what is narrated that He the Exalted laughs to His friends, i.e., His pleasure became manifest. It is said: 'the palm tree laughed' when it shows what is inside it. And it is said: 'the gardens laughed with their flowers' when they appear. And they say, when they want to exaggerate in describing the appearance of something: 'he showed his molars.' The poet said: 'A people who, when evil shows its molars, they fly to it in groups and singly, and evil has no molars in reality, but what is meant is exaggeration in its appearance, likening to one who exaggerates in his laughter so that he shows his molars.' And if it is exaggerated in that, then the interpretation of the report is: Allah the Exalted's manifestation of His pleasure and reward upon His friends.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage interprets the hadith about Allah laughing. The author provides linguistic and poetic evidence to show that laughter in Arabic can be metaphorical for manifesting pleasure. He gives examples: 'the palm tree laughed' means it revealed its fruit, 'the gardens laughed' means flowers appeared, and 'showing molars' is a hyperbolic expression for extreme manifestation. The poet's verse illustrates that 'showing molars' is not literal but an exaggeration. Therefore, the hadith means Allah manifests His pleasure and reward to His friends, not a literal laugh.

Key Terms
ضحك
الرضا
الناجذ
المبالغة
التأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah laughs literally like a human, with a sound and physical movement.
Correction: The hadith is metaphorical; it means Allah manifests His pleasure to His friends, as supported by Arabic linguistic and poetic evidence.

Checkpoint
What does the author say is the correct interpretation of the hadith about Allah laughing?
A) Allah laughs with a literal sound
B) It means Allah shows His pleasure
C) It is a metaphor for anger
D) The hadith is not authentic

Key Takeaway
The hadith of Allah's laughter is interpreted metaphorically as the manifestation of His pleasure upon His friends.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Allah 'laughs' to His friends, do you catch yourself imagining a human-like expression? How does the lesson's explanation challenge you to purify your concept of Allah, and what specific step will you take this week to ensure your thoughts about Him are free of human limitations?
?
The poet describes people rushing to evil when it 'shows its molars.' What 'molars' of sin or distraction are plainly showing in your life right now—habits, entertainments, or relationships that you know displease Allah? Will you flee from them today, or will you linger until they consume you?
?
Sanusi uses everyday Arabic idioms to show that words can carry deeper meanings. In your daily speech, do you use language carelessly, even about Allah? Are there phrases you utter—like 'Allah willing' without intention—that need to be purified? Commit to one specific change in how you speak about Allah starting now.

The Fallacious Argument for Applying 'Jism' to Allah
Nasafi introduces the debate over applying 'jism' to Allah and criticizes the agency analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that Allah must be a body because He is an agent is invalid and how Nasafi exposes the hidden corporealist agenda behind such claims.

Core Idea
Claiming Allah is a body because He is an agent is a flawed analogy ignoring divine transcendence.

In this passage, Nasafi addresses those who assert that the term 'jism' can be applied to Allah by reasoning from His attributes. They argue that in the physical world, only bodies are agents, so the Divine Agent must also be a body. Nasafi exposes this reasoning as superficial and reveals the true intention behind such claims—to conceal a corporealist theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the argument being refuted
Find the part where the opponents claim Allah is an agent and infer that He must be a body. Notice the analogy: only a body is an agent in the witnessed world.
This is a common sectarian argument from the Hishamiyya and others.
3
Understand Nasafi's suspicion
Nasafi suggests these opponents actually intend corporeality but hide it because their refutation forces them to reinterpret 'jism' as existence or self-subsistence.
This shows the importance of knowing the real motives behind theological claims.
4
Reflect on the weakness of the analogy
Consider why the analogy from the witnessed world to the unseen is problematic. The Divine is not comparable to created bodies in all aspects.
This lesson sets the stage for the linguistic proof in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعضهم على القائم بالذات على ما مر فنتكلم فيه كما نتكلم في سائر الأسامي اللغوية عند اختلاف أرباب اللسان فيها. أن هذا على ماذا يقع؟ وكثير من هؤلاء يتعلقون من حيث المعنى فيقولون: إنه تعالى فاعل ولا فاعل إلا الجسم في الشاهد فكذا في الغائب، وكذا يستدلون بكونه حيا قادرا عالما سميعا بصيرا على حسب ما تعلقت به الطائفة الأولى، وليس لهؤلاء شبهة في المسألة يتعلقون بها، وظني أن مراد هؤلاء من إطلاق هذا الاسم إثبات ما هو معناه في الجثة والتركيب غير أن إبطال خصومهم كلامهم على وجه يمكنهم إنكار ذلك ألجأهم إلى أن ظهروا أنهم غير مريدين بهذا الإطلاق ذلك المعنى، وإنما يريدون به ما يمكنهم بصحيحه من المعنى الثابت كالوجود، والقيام بالذات، وصحة كونه فاعلا، وتأولوا كلام سلفهم بهذا ليستروا بذلك معايب من اتخذوه إماما لهم، أما الدليل على هذا: أن الكرامية زعموا أنه تعالى مماس لجميع الصفحة العليا من العرش مع سعتها وتفضل ذاته عن تلك الصفحة من الجهات الأربع، ولم يتصور ذلك إلا في متركب. ثم نبين فساد قولهم في هذا فنقول : إن الوجود والقيام بالذات لن يتصور فيها التزايد والتفاوت، فلا تجري فيها المبالغة والتفضيل إذ جريانهما من خصائص ما له تزايد في نفسه ألا ترى أن العلم لما كان متزايدا في نفسه يجري فيه المبالغة فيقال: هو عليم. ويجري فيه التفضيل فيقال : زيد أعلم من عمرو؟ وكذا القدرة والسمع والبصر، والقيام بالذات، والوجود ( والقيام بالنفس ) لما كان لا يجري فيها التزايد يجري فيهما المبالغة فلا يقال فيه فعيل ولا فعال ولا التفضيل، فلا يقال أفعل، وأنه لا يقال : هذا أوجد من ذاك ولا أقوم بالذات منه، وحيث رأينا أنهم يقولون عن كثرة الأجزاء: هذا جسيم على طريق المبالغة، وعند التفضيل يقولون: هذا أجسم من ذلك. دل أنه ليس باسم لما لا تزايد له في نفسه وهو الوجود، والقيام بالذات بل هو اسم لما له تزايد، وذلك هو كثرة الأجزاء لا معنى آخر متزايد فإن لفظة المبالغة والتفضيل من الجسم يدوران وجودا وعدما مع كثرة الأجزاء لا مع غيرها من المعاني ( فعلم أنه اسم له كالمبالغة بلفظ العلم والتفضيل بلفظة أعلم لما كانا يدوران مع العلم وجودا وعدما لا مع غيره من المعاني ) علم أن العالم مأخوذ من العلم فكذا هذا ، وكذا القدير وأقدر من القدرة ، وكذا في سائر الصفات.
'Some of them [affirm that He is] self-subsisting (qāʼim bi al-dhāt) as has passed, so we speak about it just as we speak about the rest of the linguistic names when the speakers of the language differ about it. What is this based on? Many of these [people] cling to meaning (al-maʻnā) and say: He, exalted, is an agent (fāʻil), and there is no agent except the body (al-jism) in the witnessed world, so likewise in the unseen. Similarly they infer from His being living, powerful, knowing, hearing, seeing, according to what the first group clung to. These have no specious argument in the issue to cling to. My opinion is that the intention of these [people] in applying this name is to affirm its meaning as corporeality (juththa) and composition (tarkīb), except that their opponents refute their speech in a way that they can deny it forced them to show that they do not intend that meaning by this application, but rather intend by it what they can, by their [own] proof, of the established meaning such as existence (al-wujūd), self-subsistence (al-qiyām bi al-dhāt), and the validity of being an agent. They interpret the speech of their predecessors with this to conceal thereby the defects of him whom they took as their leader. As for the proof of this: the Karramiyya claimed that He, exalted, is in contact (mumāss) with all the upper surface of the Throne despite its vastness, and His essence extends beyond that surface from the four directions, and this can only be conceived in something composed (murakkab). Then we show the corruption of their statement in this and say: Existence and self-subsistence cannot admit of increase (tazāyud) and disparity (tafāwut), so intensification (mubālagha) and comparison (tafḍīl) do not apply to them, since their application is among the characteristics of what has increase in itself. Do you not see that knowledge, since it increases in itself, intensification applies to it, so it is said: He is knowing (ʻalīm); and comparison applies to it, so it is said: Zayd is more knowing (aʻlam) than ʻAmr? Likewise power, hearing, sight, self-subsistence, and existence—since increase does not apply to them, intensification does not apply, so one does not say [for them] faʻīl or faʻʻāl, nor comparison, so one does say afʻal. And it is not said: 'This is more existent (awjad) than that' nor 'more self-subsistent (aqwam bi al-dhāt) than him'. Since we see that they say, concerning abundance of parts: 'This is huge (jasīm)' by way of intensification, and in comparison they say: 'This is huger (ajsam) than that', it indicates that [the word jism] is not a name for what has no increase in itself—namely existence and self-subsistence—but rather it is a name for what has increase, which is abundance of parts (kathrat al-ajzāʼ), not another increasing meaning. For the expression of intensification and comparison from 'jism' revolve in existence and non-existence with abundance of parts, not with other meanings. Thus it is known that it is a name for it, just as the intensification by the word ʻalīm and the comparison by the word aʻlam, since they revolve with knowledge in existence and non-existence, not with other meanings. It is known that ʻālim is derived from ʻilm, and so this; likewise al-qadīr and aqdar are from qudra, and likewise in the rest of the attributes.'
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who claim that the word 'jism' (body) is applied to Allah in a valid sense. The author begins by noting that some people (the Hishamiyya and others) assert that Allah is self-subsisting (qāʼim bi al-dhāt) and then argue over the applicability of the term 'jism', treating it like other linguistic names where speakers differ. He then describes how many of them argue from a 'meaning' perspective: they claim that Allah is an agent, and in the witnessed world only a body can be an agent, so in the unseen it must be similar. They also infer from attributes like living, powerful, knowing, hearing, seeing. The author dismisses these as having no real substance. His own analysis is that these people actually intend to affirm corporeality and composition, but because their opponents refute their arguments in a way they cannot openly deny, they pretend not to intend that meaning and instead reinterpret it to refer to existence, self-subsistence, and agency, thereby covering up the faults of their leaders. The proof is then given: the Karramiyya explicitly claim that Allah is in contact (mumāss) with the entire upper surface of the Throne and that His essence extends beyond it in all directions—this can only be true of something composite (murakkab). The author then explains why this is corrupt. He argues that existence and self-subsistence do not admit of increase (tazāyud) or disparity, so they cannot be described with intensification (mubālagha) or comparison (tafḍīl). These linguistic forms (like ʻalīm/aʻlam) are characteristics of things that can increase. For example, knowledge increases, so we say 'He is Knowing' (ʻalīm) and 'Zayd is more knowing' (aʻlam). But existence and self-subsistence do not increase, so we do not say 'more existent' or 'more self-subsistent'. In contrast, for bodies we say 'huge' (jasīm) and 'huger' (ajsam) precisely because abundance of parts allows increase. Therefore, the term 'jism' denotes something that increases (abundance of parts), not something that does not increase like existence. The author concludes by drawing an analogy: just as ʻālim is derived from ʻilm (knowledge), so the form jasīm/ajsam is derived from jism as an expression of increase in parts. The same applies to qadīr/aqdar from power (qudra) and all similar attributes.

Key Terms
جسم (jism)
قائم بالذات (qāʼim bi al-dhāt)
فاعل (fāʻil)
شاهد (shāhid)
غائب (ghāʼib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah can be called a 'body' metaphorically because He is an agent.
Correction: Nasafi argues that 'jism' always implies composition and increase, which cannot apply to Allah, and the agency analogy is a false inference from the seen to the unseen.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the real intention of those who claim Allah is a body?
A) To affirm that Allah has existence
B) To affirm that Allah is an agent
C) To affirm that Allah has corporeality and composition
D) To affirm that Allah is self-subsisting

Key Takeaway
The argument that Allah is a body because He is an agent is based on a false analogy and fails to consider the unique nature of the Divine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defended a belief about Allah that you knew deep down was based on weak reasoning, just to fit in with a group? How does Nasafi's exposure of the opponents' hidden corporealism challenge you to be honest about your own theology?
?
When you think of Allah, do you ever unconsciously imagine a body or form? This passage warns that even claiming Allah is an 'agent' like a body can lead to a flawed understanding. Are you willing to strip away any mental images you have of Him, even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
The opponents used an analogy from the witnessed world to understand the unseen. Are there any analogies you rely on to understand Allah that might be equally flawed? Ask yourself: Am I using my intellect to transcend creation, or am I dragging Allah down to my level?

The Linguistic Proof: 'Jism' Denotes Increase in Parts
Nasafi argues from linguistic usage that 'jism' means abundance of parts, not existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic argument that the word 'jism' must refer to abundance of parts because it admits intensification and comparison, which are only possible for things that increase.

Core Idea
The words 'jasīm' and 'ajsam' prove that 'jism' means abundant parts, not mere existence.

After exposing the flawed analogy, Nasafi provides a decisive linguistic proof. He points out that the forms of intensification (mubālagha) and comparison (tafḍīl) are used with 'jism'—such as 'jasīm' (huge) and 'ajsam' (huger)—indicating that it denotes something that can increase. But existence and self-subsistence do not increase, so 'jism' cannot mean those. Instead, it refers to abundance of parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the Karramiyya claim
Find where the Karramiyya assert that Allah is in contact with the Throne. Nasafi says this implies composition, which is the starting point for the linguistic argument.
This shows the practical consequence of believing Allah is a body.
3
Understand the principle of intensification
Nasafi argues that mubālagha and tafḍīl only apply to things that admit increase. Existence and self-subsistence do not increase, so these forms cannot be used for them.
Example: we say 'alīm' (knowing) because knowledge increases, but not 'awjad' (more existent).
4
Apply the principle to 'jism'
Since we have 'jasīm' and 'ajsam', 'jism' must denote something that increases, i.e., abundance of parts.
This is the core of the proof.
5
See the analogy with 'ʿilm'
Just as 'ʿālim' is derived from 'ʿilm' because mubālagha/tafdīl revolve with knowledge, so 'jism' is derived from abundance of parts.
This reinforces the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعضهم على القائم بالذات على ما مر فنتكلم فيه كما نتكلم في سائر الأسامي اللغوية عند اختلاف أرباب اللسان فيها. أن هذا على ماذا يقع؟ وكثير من هؤلاء يتعلقون من حيث المعنى فيقولون: إنه تعالى فاعل ولا فاعل إلا الجسم في الشاهد فكذا في الغائب، وكذا يستدلون بكونه حيا قادرا عالما سميعا بصيرا على حسب ما تعلقت به الطائفة الأولى، وليس لهؤلاء شبهة في المسألة يتعلقون بها، وظني أن مراد هؤلاء من إطلاق هذا الاسم إثبات ما هو معناه في الجثة والتركيب غير أن إبطال خصومهم كلامهم على وجه يمكنهم إنكار ذلك ألجأهم إلى أن ظهروا أنهم غير مريدين بهذا الإطلاق ذلك المعنى، وإنما يريدون به ما يمكنهم بصحيحه من المعنى الثابت كالوجود، والقيام بالذات، وصحة كونه فاعلا، وتأولوا كلام سلفهم بهذا ليستروا بذلك معايب من اتخذوه إماما لهم، أما الدليل على هذا: أن الكرامية زعموا أنه تعالى مماس لجميع الصفحة العليا من العرش مع سعتها وتفضل ذاته عن تلك الصفحة من الجهات الأربع، ولم يتصور ذلك إلا في متركب. ثم نبين فساد قولهم في هذا فنقول : إن الوجود والقيام بالذات لن يتصور فيها التزايد والتفاوت، فلا تجري فيها المبالغة والتفضيل إذ جريانهما من خصائص ما له تزايد في نفسه ألا ترى أن العلم لما كان متزايدا في نفسه يجري فيه المبالغة فيقال: هو عليم. ويجري فيه التفضيل فيقال : زيد أعلم من عمرو؟ وكذا القدرة والسمع والبصر، والقيام بالذات، والوجود ( والقيام بالنفس ) لما كان لا يجري فيها التزايد يجري فيهما المبالغة فلا يقال فيه فعيل ولا فعال ولا التفضيل، فلا يقال أفعل، وأنه لا يقال : هذا أوجد من ذاك ولا أقوم بالذات منه، وحيث رأينا أنهم يقولون عن كثرة الأجزاء: هذا جسيم على طريق المبالغة، وعند التفضيل يقولون: هذا أجسم من ذلك. دل أنه ليس باسم لما لا تزايد له في نفسه وهو الوجود، والقيام بالذات بل هو اسم لما له تزايد، وذلك هو كثرة الأجزاء لا معنى آخر متزايد فإن لفظة المبالغة والتفضيل من الجسم يدوران وجودا وعدما مع كثرة الأجزاء لا مع غيرها من المعاني ( فعلم أنه اسم له كالمبالغة بلفظ العلم والتفضيل بلفظة أعلم لما كانا يدوران مع العلم وجودا وعدما لا مع غيره من المعاني ) علم أن العالم مأخوذ من العلم فكذا هذا ، وكذا القدير وأقدر من القدرة ، وكذا في سائر الصفات.
'Some of them [affirm that He is] self-subsisting (qāʼim bi al-dhāt) as has passed, so we speak about it just as we speak about the rest of the linguistic names when the speakers of the language differ about it. What is this based on? Many of these [people] cling to meaning (al-maʻnā) and say: He, exalted, is an agent (fāʻil), and there is no agent except the body (al-jism) in the witnessed world, so likewise in the unseen. Similarly they infer from His being living, powerful, knowing, hearing, seeing, according to what the first group clung to. These have no specious argument in the issue to cling to. My opinion is that the intention of these [people] in applying this name is to affirm its meaning as corporeality (juththa) and composition (tarkīb), except that their opponents refute their speech in a way that they can deny it forced them to show that they do not intend that meaning by this application, but rather intend by it what they can, by their [own] proof, of the established meaning such as existence (al-wujūd), self-subsistence (al-qiyām bi al-dhāt), and the validity of being an agent. They interpret the speech of their predecessors with this to conceal thereby the defects of him whom they took as their leader. As for the proof of this: the Karramiyya claimed that He, exalted, is in contact (mumāss) with all the upper surface of the Throne despite its vastness, and His essence extends beyond that surface from the four directions, and this can only be conceived in something composed (murakkab). Then we show the corruption of their statement in this and say: Existence and self-subsistence cannot admit of increase (tazāyud) and disparity (tafāwut), so intensification (mubālagha) and comparison (tafḍīl) do not apply to them, since their application is among the characteristics of what has increase in itself. Do you not see that knowledge, since it increases in itself, intensification applies to it, so it is said: He is knowing (ʻalīm); and comparison applies to it, so it is said: Zayd is more knowing (aʻlam) than ʻAmr? Likewise power, hearing, sight, self-subsistence, and existence—since increase does not apply to them, intensification does not apply, so one does not say [for them] faʻīl or faʻʻāl, nor comparison, so one does say afʻal. And it is not said: 'This is more existent (awjad) than that' nor 'more self-subsistent (aqwam bi al-dhāt) than him'. Since we see that they say, concerning abundance of parts: 'This is huge (jasīm)' by way of intensification, and in comparison they say: 'This is huger (ajsam) than that', it indicates that [the word jism] is not a name for what has no increase in itself—namely existence and self-subsistence—but rather it is a name for what has increase, which is abundance of parts (kathrat al-ajzāʼ), not another increasing meaning. For the expression of intensification and comparison from 'jism' revolve in existence and non-existence with abundance of parts, not with other meanings. Thus it is known that it is a name for it, just as the intensification by the word ʻalīm and the comparison by the word aʻlam, since they revolve with knowledge in existence and non-existence, not with other meanings. It is known that ʻālim is derived from ʻilm, and so this; likewise al-qadīr and aqdar are from qudra, and likewise in the rest of the attributes.'
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who claim that the word 'jism' (body) is applied to Allah in a valid sense. The author begins by noting that some people (the Hishamiyya and others) assert that Allah is self-subsisting (qāʼim bi al-dhāt) and then argue over the applicability of the term 'jism', treating it like other linguistic names where speakers differ. He then describes how many of them argue from a 'meaning' perspective: they claim that Allah is an agent, and in the witnessed world only a body can be an agent, so in the unseen it must be similar. They also infer from attributes like living, powerful, knowing, hearing, seeing. The author dismisses these as having no real substance. His own analysis is that these people actually intend to affirm corporeality and composition, but because their opponents refute their arguments in a way they cannot openly deny, they pretend not to intend that meaning and instead reinterpret it to refer to existence, self-subsistence, and agency, thereby covering up the faults of their leaders. The proof is then given: the Karramiyya explicitly claim that Allah is in contact (mumāss) with the entire upper surface of the Throne and that His essence extends beyond it in all directions—this can only be true of something composite (murakkab). The author then explains why this is corrupt. He argues that existence and self-subsistence do not admit of increase (tazāyud) or disparity, so they cannot be described with intensification (mubālagha) or comparison (tafḍīl). These linguistic forms (like ʻalīm/aʻlam) are characteristics of things that can increase. For example, knowledge increases, so we say 'He is Knowing' (ʻalīm) and 'Zayd is more knowing' (aʻlam). But existence and self-subsistence do not increase, so we do not say 'more existent' or 'more self-subsistent'. In contrast, for bodies we say 'huge' (jasīm) and 'huger' (ajsam) precisely because abundance of parts allows increase. Therefore, the term 'jism' denotes something that increases (abundance of parts), not something that does not increase like existence. The author concludes by drawing an analogy: just as ʻālim is derived from ʻilm (knowledge), so the form jasīm/ajsam is derived from jism as an expression of increase in parts. The same applies to qadīr/aqdar from power (qudra) and all similar attributes.

Key Terms
جسيم (jasīm)
أجسم (ajsam)
تزايد (tazāyud)
مبالغة (mubālagha)
تفضيل (tafḍīl)
كثرة الأجزاء (kathrat al-ajzāʼ)
وجود (wujūd)
قيام بالذات (qiyām bi al-dhāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'jism' is a synonym for 'existence' and can be applied to Allah.
Correction: Nasafi shows that 'jism' always implies increase and composition, as evidenced by its forms of intensification and comparison, which cannot be used for simple existence.

Checkpoint
What does the existence of the words 'جسيم' (jasīm) and 'أجسم' (ajsam) indicate about the meaning of 'جسم' (jism)?
A) It indicates existence
B) It indicates self-subsistence
C) It indicates abundance of parts
D) It indicates agency

Key Takeaway
The linguistic behavior of 'jism' forces the conclusion that it means abundance of parts, not mere existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is beyond comparison', does your speech truly reflect a heart that has given up all spatial and physical images of Him? In what quiet moments do you still find yourself imagining a 'big' God in a 'place'? What would it take for your inner thoughts to be as precise as this linguistic proof demands?
?
The Karramiyya found comfort in a God who could be 'in contact' with the Throne. Why do you think we are drawn to a God we can mentally 'locate'? How does this lesson expose a subtle idolatry in your own soul? Ask Allah to purify your understanding of His transcendence.
?
This proof uses the very structure of language to purify our belief. Are you willing to let the Arabic words you use about Allah—like 'jism' or 'makān'—be governed by the same logical rigor? Which common phrases about Allah do you use that might unintentionally imply limitation? Will you commit to retraining your tongue and heart today?

Refuting the Jasm-Jism Argument
Refutes the claim that God is called jism based on jasm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the metaphorical use of 'jasm' (great) does not justify calling Allah 'jism' (body).

Core Idea
Metaphorical hyperbole does not justify literal bodily attributes for God.

The opponents of Islamic theology sometimes argue that since the word 'jasm' (great) is used for great matters, God can be called 'jism' (body). In this passage, the author refutes this by showing that 'jasm' is a metaphorical hyperbole implying composition, which cannot apply to God, and that the word actually used is 'jasm', not 'jism', creating a logical contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the poetic quote and proverb used as evidence by the opponents.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponents use the phrase 'jasmīmāt al-umūr' (great matters) to claim that God can be called 'jism' (body). Identify this inference.
3
Understand the refutation
The author explains that 'jasm' is used metaphorically as hyperbole for greatness, likening great matters to bodies with many parts. This does not mean the matters are literally bodies.
Metaphor (majāz) is key here.
4
Recognize the logical contradiction
The author points out that the opponents themselves agree 'jism' is not used for God in a praiseworthy sense. Thus, what is used for exaltation (jasm) they disallow, and what they allow (jism) is never used for exaltation.
This contradiction invalidates the inference.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument collapses, affirming that Allah is not a body. Consider how this illustrates the importance of precise terminology in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا تركب للأمر لما أنه عرض قال جرير يخاطب عمر بن عبد العزيز: حملت أمرا عظيما فاصطبرت له وسرت فيه بأمر الله يا ويقال: جسيمات الأمور مشوبة بالمكاره، قلنا: هذا مستعمل على طريق المجاز تشبيها لما عظم من الأمور بما كثر أجزاؤه من الأجسام، إذ هذه لفظة مبالغة تنبئ عن كثرة في التركيب ومثل هذا لا يجوز إطلاقه على الله تعالى على أن المستعمل في هذا لفظة الجسيم لا لفظة الجسم، فإنهم ما استعملوا هذه اللفظة قط في معنى التعظيم فأنتم ساعدتمونا على امتناع لفظة الجسم في أسمائه، فما استعمل في معنى التعظيم، لا تجوزون إطلاقه، وما تجوزون إطلاقه لا يستعمل للتعظيم البتة؛ فبطل الاستدلال والله الموفق.
Do not ride for the matter because it is an accident. Jarīr said addressing ʿUmar ibn ʿAbd al-ʿAzīz: 'You carried a great matter and endured it, and you proceeded in it by the command of God, O...' And it is said: 'Great matters (jasmīmāt al-umūr) are mixed with hardships.' We say: This is used metaphorically, likening what is great of matters to what has many parts among bodies, for this is a word of hyperbole indicating abundance in composition. And such is not permissible to apply to God Almighty, especially since what is used in this is the word 'jasm' (great), not the word 'jism' (body). For they never used this word in the sense of exaltation. So you have helped us against the permissibility of the word 'jism' among His names. That which is used in the sense of exaltation, you do not permit to apply, and that which you permit to apply is never used for exaltation. Thus the inference is invalid, and God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an inference made by opponents who claim that God can be called 'jism' (body) based on linguistic usage where 'jasm' (great) is used for great matters. The author begins by citing a poetic line and a proverb that use 'jasm' for great matters. He then explains that this is metaphorical (majāz), likening great matters to bodies with many parts, and it is a hyperbolic expression implying composition. Such an expression cannot be applied to God. Moreover, the word actually used is 'jasm', not 'jism'. The opponents themselves agree that 'jism' is not used for God in a praiseworthy sense. Thus, the inference is contradictory: what is used for exaltation (jasm) they disallow applying to God, and what they allow (jism) is never used for exaltation. Hence the argument collapses.

Key Terms
جسم (jism)
جسيم (jasm)
مجاز (majāz)
استدلال (istidlāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'jasm' (great) is equivalent to 'jism' (body) and can be used to prove God is a body.
Correction: 'Jasm' is a metaphorical hyperbole for greatness, not a literal description of being a body. The two words are distinct, and the argument is self-contradictory.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the argument that God can be called jism based on the phrase 'jasmīmāt al-umūr'?
A) Because jasm is a synonym for jism in all contexts.
B) Because jasm is used metaphorically and hyperbolically, not literally as a body.
C) Because the phrase is a lie.
D) Because jasm means 'small'.

Key Takeaway
The linguistic argument for calling God a body is invalid because the word used for greatness (jasm) is metaphorical and not equivalent to the word for body (jism).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God's attributes, do you ever rely on human language that might inadvertently limit Him? In this lesson, the opponents used a familiar expression to argue for God's body. Is there an attribute you hold about God that you have never questioned because it 'makes sense' to you? What would it cost you to admit that your mental image of God might be incomplete or even wrong?
?
The author refutes an argument by showing that the word used for greatness (jasm) is not the same as the word for body (jism). Are there areas in your faith where you conflate similar-sounding concepts without careful distinction? How can you train yourself to be more precise with Islamic terminology, even when it challenges a comfortable belief?

The Meaning of 'Not Like Things' in Denying Bodily Attributes
Nasafi clarifies that 'not like things' negates corporeality and accidentally from Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the phrase 'لا كالأشياء' (not like things) is a complete negation of corporeality and accidentally from Allah, not a concession that He might be a body unlike others.

Core Idea
'Not like things' does not leave room—it negates body and accident entirely.

Imam Nasafi now takes a common theological phrase and clarifies its precise meaning. Some might think that saying 'Allah is not like things' allows for the possibility that He is a body unlike others. But the author shows that this phrase is actually a complete negation of the very meaning of body and accident. Let's see how he argues this.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise and forceful style of Imam Nasafi.
2
Identify what is being negated
The passage states that corporeality and accidentally are impossible for the Eternal. Then it clarifies that 'not like things' intends to drop the meaning of both from Allah.
The words 'الجسمية' (corporeality) and 'العرضية' (accidentality) are key.
3
Understand the author's critique
Imam Nasafi rebukes anyone who thinks the phrase leaves open the name 'body'. He says such a person has forced him to negate the body—and calls this the utmost stupidity.
The author uses strong language to stress the clarity of his intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مر من استحالة تحقيق معنى الجسم على القديم، وكذا العرضية، فإذا مرادنا من قولنا: لا كالأشياء إسقاط معنى الجسمية والعرضية ونفيها عنه فمن التزم تجويز (۱) إطلاق اسم الجسم بقولنا: لا كالأشياء ومرادنا من هذا اللفظ إسقاط اسم الجسم فقد ألزمنا بنفينا الجسم (۲)، وهذا نهاية في الغباوة والحمق. والله الموفق.
The impossibility of affirming the meaning of body for the Eternal has passed, and likewise the meaning of accident. So our intention in our saying 'not like things' is to drop the meaning of corporeality and accidentally and to negate them from Him. So whoever insists on permitting the application of the name 'body' due to our saying 'not like things', while our intention from this expression is to negate the name 'body', he has necessitated for us the negation of the body. And this is the utmost in stupidity and foolishness. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is a clarification and rebuttal. It explains the intended meaning of the phrase 'لا كالأشياء' (not like things) in negating bodily attributes from God. The author states that it has already been established that it is impossible to affirm the meaning of body (جسم) or accident (عرض) for the Eternal. Therefore, when they say 'not like things,' their purpose is to negate the reality of corporeality and accidentally from God. The one who interprets this phrase as allowing the application of the name 'body' has misunderstood, because the very phrase is meant to negate the name. Such a mistaken interpretation is described as the utmost stupidity.

Misconception: That the phrase 'not like things' leaves open the possibility that God is a body unlike other bodies.
Correction: The author clarifies that the phrase negates corporeality altogether, not just comparison.

B) Negate the meaning of corporeality and accidentally

D) Affirm similarity to accidents

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'لا كالأشياء' negates the very concepts of body and accident from Allah, not merely comparison.

Proof of the Impossibility of Describing God with Form, Color, Taste, etc.
Imam Nasafi proves that God cannot be described with form, color, taste, smell, and other composite qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for why God cannot be described with form, color, taste, or other composite sensible qualities.

Core Idea
God's simplicity rules out all forms and sensible qualities, as they entail composition and mutual contradiction.

In the previous lesson, it was established that God is not composite. Imam Nasafi now builds on that to show that form, color, taste, and similar attributes are impossible for God. He presents a rigorous argument based on the mutual exclusivity of forms and the lack of any basis to prefer one over another.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a heading and then presents a logical proof.
2
Identify the main argument for form
The passage argues that form is impossible because it is composition itself, and because forms are mutually contradictory. Understand why no form can be preferred.
The author contrasts this with attributes like knowledge, power, etc., which are perfections.
3
Understand the contrast with other attributes
Unlike form, attributes like knowledge are indicated by creation and their opposites are deficiencies. This shows why those attributes are affirmed without their opposites.
4
Extend the reasoning to other qualities
The author concludes that color, taste, smell, etc., are also impossible for the same reason: they all entail composition and arbitrariness.
This is a direct application of the same principle.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how this proof strengthens the doctrine of tanzih (transcendence) and why God's attributes must be understood in a way befitting His majesty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في بيان استحالة وصف الله تعالى بالصورة واللون والطعم والرائحة وغير ذلك وإذا ثبت بما مر ذكره أنه تعالى ليس بمتركب دل أنه يستحيل عليه الصورة لأنها هي التركب، ولأن الصور مختلفة واجتماعها عليه مستحيل (۳)، لتنافيها في أنفسها وليس البعض بأولى من البعض لاستواء كل في إفادة المدح أو النقص وانعدام دلالة المحدثات عليه بخلاف العلم والقدرة والحياة والإرادة والسمع والبصر مع أضدادها فإنها يتعلق بها المدح فكانت من صفات الكمال التي شرط (٤) القدم، ويتعلق بأضدادها النقيصة التي هي من أمارات الحدوث، وكذا المحدثات تدل على هذه الصفات لا على أضدادها فلم توجد المساواة بينها وبين أضدادها في الثبوت فثبتت هي دون أضدادها، بخلاف الصورة فإنها كلها في جواز الثبوت على السواء ألا ترى أن من ادعى ثبوت بعضها لم يكن بأولى ممن يدعي ثبوت غير ذلك، ولا وجه إلى ثبوت كلها لمكان التنافي، فلو اختص شيء منها بالثبوت ثبت بتخصيص مخصص ولا وجه إليه لما مر، والله الموفق. ولهذه النكتة ادعينا استحالة اتصاف الباري جل وعلا باللون والطعم والرائحة والحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة فافهم (۱)، والله الموفق.
Chapter on explaining the impossibility of describing God Almighty with form, color, taste, smell, and other than that. And when it is established by what has been mentioned that He Almighty is not composite, it indicates that form is impossible for Him because it is composition itself. And because forms are different and their coming together upon Him is impossible (3), due to their mutual contradiction; and some are not more deserving than others because all are equal in conveying praise or deficiency and the lack of indication of originated things upon Him—unlike knowledge, power, life, will, hearing, and sight along with their opposites, for praise attaches to them so they are among the attributes of perfection that require (4) eternity, while deficiency attaches to their opposites which are signs of temporality. And likewise, originated things indicate these attributes and not their opposites, so there is no equality between them and their opposites in affirmation; thus these are affirmed without their opposites. Unlike form, for all forms are equal in the possibility of affirmation. Do you not see that whoever claims the affirmation of some of them is not more deserving than whoever claims the affirmation of others? And there is no way to affirm all of them due to mutual contradiction. So if some of them were specifically affirmed, that would be by specification of a specifier, and there is no way to that for what has passed. And Allah is the Grantor of success. And for this subtle point, we claim the impossibility of the description of the Exalted and Glorious with color, taste, smell, heat, cold, moisture, and dryness. So understand (1). And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the impossibility of God having form, color, taste, etc. It begins by stating the heading. Then the argument: Since God is not composite (مركب), He cannot have form because form is composition itself. The author then argues that all forms are mutually contradictory; therefore it is impossible for God to have any specific form because no form is more deserving of affirmation than another. This contrasts with attributes like knowledge, power, etc., which are perfections and are indicated by creation, whereas their opposites are deficiencies and signs of temporality. Hence God is affirmed with the positive attributes without their opposites. But with forms, all are equal in possibility, so there is no preference. If one form were chosen, it would require an arbitrary specification which is not possible. Therefore, form is impossible. Then the author extends this to other sensible qualities like color, taste, etc., concluding they are impossible.

تركيب
صورة
أضداد
صفات الكمال
اختصاص
تخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God could possess a form different from creatures.
Correction: The author argues form inherently entails composition, which is impossible for God. Additionally, forms are mutually contradictory and cannot be preferred without arbitrary specification.

Checkpoint

A) Because forms are invisible
B) Because forms are composite by nature and contradict each other
C) Because forms are temporal
D) Because forms cannot be known

Key Takeaway
God is free from all forms and sensible qualities because they entail composition and arbitrary specification, which contradicts His necessary existence.

Two Disagreements Among Deniers of Anthropomorphism
Imam Nasafi concludes refutation of tashbih and outlines two disagreements among deniers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of disagreement among those who reject anthropomorphism and why a precise definition of resemblance is necessary to resolve them.

Core Idea
Even those who reject anthropomorphism disagree about which attributes imply it.

Imam Nasafi summarizes the conclusion that God is not like any creation, then introduces an important nuance: among those who reject anthropomorphism, there are disputes about whether certain attributes actually imply resemblance or not. This passage sets the stage for defining what constitutes true resemblance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the flow from conclusion to the introduction of internal disagreement.
The passage is transitional; it recaps and then sets up a new discussion.
2
Identify the two aspects of disagreement
The passage lists two specific disagreements among those who deny tashbih. Find them and note how each side accuses the other of implying resemblance.
The two aspects are introduced with 'أحدهما' and 'الثاني'.
3
Reflect on the need for a definition
The author says resolution requires clarifying 'what constitutes resemblance'. Think about why a clear definition is necessary before debating specific attributes.
This prepares for the next section where the definition is given.
4
Note the author's method
Observe how Imam Nasafi first establishes common ground (no resemblance) then introduces internal disagreement. This is a dialectical approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إبطال التشبيه: ولما ثبت بما مر من الكلام أنه تعالى ليس بعرض ولا جوهر ولا جسم ثبت أنه لا مشابهة بين الله تعالى وبين شيء من المخلوقات، وخالفنا في ذلك القائلون بأن الله تعالى على صورة الآدمي له ما للبشر من الأعضاء - تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرا - وقد سبقت الدلالة على بطلان قولهم. ثم بعد ذلك نقول: إن المنكرين للتشبيه اختلفوا فيما بينهم، وخالف البعض البعض (۲) بإثبات البعض منهم صفة الله تعالى أو إجازة معنى عليه يقع بذلك تشبيه فحدث من ذلك بيننا وبين مخالفينا الخلاف من وجهين: أحدهما: إثبات بعضهم معاني على الله تعالى يوجب ذلك التشبيه وهم ينكرون أن يثبت بها تشبيه فيحتاج إلى إقامة الدليل أن ذلك موجب للتشبيه والثاني: أنا نثبت الله تعالى صفة ينكر بعض مخالفينا ذلك (۳) ويدعون (٤) علينا بذلك التشبيه، فيحتاج إلى إقامة الدليل أن ذلك ليس بموجب للتشبيه ولن يتوصل إلى ذلك إلا ببيان ما تقع به المشابهة فنشتغل ببيان ذلك فنقول(٥)) - وبالله التوفيق.
Chapter on the refutation of anthropomorphism: And when it is established by what has passed of discourse that He Almighty is neither an accident nor a substance nor a body, it is established that there is no resemblance between God Almighty and any of the created things. And those who say that God Almighty is in the form of Adam, having the same organs as humans—exalted is God above what the wrongdoers say with great exaltation—have opposed us in that. And the evidence for the invalidity of their statement has already preceded. Then after that we say: Those who deny anthropomorphism have differed among themselves, and some have opposed others (2) by some of them affirming an attribute for God Almighty or allowing a meaning upon Him by which resemblance occurs. So from that, a disagreement occurred between us and our opponents from two aspects: One of them: Some of them affirm meanings upon God Almighty that necessitate that resemblance, while they deny that resemblance occurs by them, so it is necessary to produce evidence that that necessitates resemblance. And the second: We affirm an attribute for God Almighty that some of our opponents deny (3) and they claim against us (4) that this is resemblance, so it is necessary to produce evidence that that does not necessitate resemblance. And that will not be achieved except by clarifying what constitutes resemblance. So we shall engage in clarifying that and we say (5)—and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is a transitional and expository section. It first states that from previous proofs that God is not an accident, substance, or body, it follows that there is no resemblance whatsoever between God and creation. It then notes that anthropomorphists (those who liken God to humans) have opposed this, but their falsehood has already been shown. Then the author introduces a further nuance: even among those who deny anthropomorphism in principle, there is disagreement. Some of them affirm attributes for God that actually imply resemblance, yet they deny that implication. Others (including the author's school) affirm certain attributes that others claim imply resemblance. This creates a two-fold debate: first, proving that certain attributes do imply resemblance (against those who deny it), and second, proving that certain attributes do not imply resemblance (against those who claim they do). The author indicates that resolving this requires defining what exactly constitutes resemblance (مشابهة). The passage sets up the need for a clear definition, which will be provided next.

Key Terms
تشبيه
مشابهة
عرض
جوهر
جسم
صورة آدمي
منكرون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All who deny anthropomorphism agree on which attributes are acceptable.
Correction: The passage shows that even among deniers, there is dispute: some attribute meanings to God that actually necessitate resemblance, while others deny that certain attributes are resemblance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two aspects of disagreement among those who deny anthropomorphism?
A) Whether God is a body or not
B) Whether certain attributes imply resemblance or not
C) Whether God has form or not
D) Whether negation of resemblance is obligatory

Key Takeaway
The internal dispute among deniers of tashbih shows that a clear definition of 'resemblance' is essential for theological argument.

Learning Objective

It states that based on earlier proofs that God is not a body or an accident, He cannot possess any physical attributes. The author ends with a directive to understand and a prayer for success.

Two Aspects of Disagreement over Tashbih
Restates proof of God's incorporeality and introduces two aspects of disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why even among deniers of tashbih there is internal disagreement and how two specific aspects of conflict arise.

Core Idea
Denying tashbih is not enough; the definition of similarity is key.

Having established that God is not a body, the author now turns to a more subtle challenge. Even among those who explicitly reject anthropomorphism, disagreements persist about which attributes imply similarity. This lesson introduces two symmetrical aspects of that disagreement, setting the stage for a precise definition of what similarity means.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the heading 'خلاف المنكرين للتشبيه'.
2
Identify the restated conclusion
The passage begins by restating that God is not an accident, substance, or body, so no similarity exists. Find this sentence and understand its role.
This connects to earlier proofs.
3
Recognize the two aspects
The passage explicitly mentions two aspects of disagreement. Locate the phrases 'أحدهما:' and 'الثاني:' and note what each aspect concerns.
One aspect is about opponents' attributes, the other about our own.
4
Understand the need for definition
The author states that resolving both aspects requires explaining what similarity consists of. Why is a definition necessary?
This sets up the upcoming discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إبطال التشبيه ولما ثبت بما مر من الكلام أنه تعالى ليس بعرض ولا جوهر ولا جسم ثبت أنه لا مشابهة بين الله تعالى وبين شيء من المخلوقات، وخالفنا في ذلك القائلون بأن الله تعالى على صورة الآدمي له ما للبشر من الأعضاء - تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرا - وقد سبقت الدلالة على بطلان قولهم. خلاف المنكرين للتشبيه ثم بعد ذلك نقول: إن المنكرين للتشبيه اختلفوا فيما بينهم، وخالف البعض البعض بإثبات البعض منهم صفة الله تعالى أو إجازة معنى عليه يقع بذلك تشبيه فحدث من ذلك بيننا وبين مخالفينا الخلاف من وجهين: أحدهما: إثبات بعضهم معاني على الله تعالى يوجب ذلك التشبيه وهم ينكرون أن يثبت بها تشبيه فيحتاج إلى إقامة الدليل أن ذلك موجب للتشبيه والثاني: أنا نثبت الله تعالى صفة ينكر بعض مخالفينا ذلك ويدعون علينا بذلك التشبيه، فيحتاج إلى إقامة الدليل أن ذلك ليس بموجب للتشبيه ولن يتوصل إلى ذلك إلا ببيان ما تقع به المشابهة فنشتغل ببيان ذلك فنقول: وبالله التوفيق.
Discourse on the refutation of anthropomorphism. And since it was established by the preceding speech that the Exalted is not an accident, nor a substance, nor a body, it is established that there is no similarity between God Most High and any of the creatures. And those who say that God Most High is in the form of Adam, having what humans have of limbs, opposed us in that—exalted is God above what the wrongdoers say, greatly exalted—and the proof of the invalidity of their statement has preceded. The disagreement of the deniers of anthropomorphism. Then after that we say: Indeed, the deniers of anthropomorphism differed among themselves, and some opposed others by some of them affirming an attribute of God Most High or permitting a meaning upon Him through which anthropomorphism occurs. Thus arose between us and our opponents disagreement from two aspects: One of them: Some of them affirm meanings upon God Most High that necessitate anthropomorphism, while they deny that anthropomorphism is established by them; so there is need to establish the proof that that necessitates anthropomorphism. The second: We affirm an attribute of God Most High that some of our opponents deny, and they accuse us thereby of anthropomorphism; so there is need to establish the proof that that does not necessitate anthropomorphism. And that cannot be reached except by explaining what similarity consists in, so we occupy ourselves with explaining that, and we say: And with God is success.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage begins by restating the previous conclusion that God is not an accident, substance, or body, and therefore there is no similarity between God and creation. The author notes that some (the anthropomorphists) opposed this by claiming God has a human form, but their view has already been refuted. Then, under the heading 'Disagreement of the Deniers of Anthropomorphism', the author explains that even among those who deny tashbih, there is internal disagreement. This creates two aspects of conflict between us (the author's school) and these opponents. First, some opponents affirm attributes of God that logically imply similarity, yet they deny that this constitutes tashbih; we need to prove that it does. Second, we affirm an attribute of God which opponents deny and claim it implies tashbih; we need to prove that it does not. Both issues require a clear definition of what similarity is. Therefore, the author states he will now explain that definition. This sets up the following discussion.

The Ash'ari Definition of Similitude and Its Proof
Nasafi presents the Ash'ari definition of similitude and proof via indications and refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of similitude (tamāthul) as mutual substitutability and the rational proof that establishes it.

Core Idea
Similitude is mutual replaceability, not just shared attributes.

In this passage, Nasafi presents the Ash'ari school's precise definition of similitude (tamāthul) and its proof. The definition centers on mutual substitutability: two things are similars if each can take the place of the other. This lesson unpacks the definition, its two supporting indications, the opposition between difference and similitude, and the refutation of alternative explanations.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic and translation of the definition, noting the key phrase 'يسد مسد صاحبه'.
This phrase is the essence of the Ash'ari criterion.
2
Understand the two indications
Identify the two 'indications' (dalāla) that support the definition. The first ties similitude to difference; the second ties it to substitutability.
3
Examine the black/white example
See how the example of blackness and whiteness shows that shared general attributes do not make similars; only substitutability does.
4
Grasp the opposition between difference and similitude
Understand that difference and similitude are like contraries and cannot coexist. This is a key logical principle.
5
Follow the refutation of alternatives
The passage refutes other explanations for why two things might be similars, concluding that only the given definition remains valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن الناس قد اختلفوا في ذلك فزعم الأشعري ومن تابعه أن المشتبهين والمثلين هما غيران يسد كل واحد منهما مسد صاحبه، ودلالة تقيد الحد بالمغايرة أن الشيء لا يشبه نفسه ولا يماثله فدل أن ذلك جار بين المتغايرين ودلالة قولهم: يسد مسده فإن ما لا يسد مسد صاحبه لا يعد مثلا له وإن كانت بينهما موافقة في أوصاف كثيرة كالسواد مع البياض فإنهما ليسا بمثلين وإن كان كل واحد منهما موجودا، وعرضا ولونا، فدل ارتفاع المماثلة بين هذين اللونين مع حصول الموافقة بينهما فيما بينا من الأوصاف على أن لا مماثلة مع ثبوت المخالفة بوجه من الوجوه إذ عند ثبوت المخالفة لا يسد أحدهما مسد صاحبه، وهذه هي النكتة في وهي بين المخالفة والمماثلة من التقابل ما بين المتضادين فلا يتصور أن اجتماعهما في محل واحد، فكان من ضرورة ثبوت المخالفة انتفاء المماثلة، ولأن سير الأحوال يظهر صحة هذا الحد، فإن المتماثلين ما كانا متماثلين لكونهما جوهرين لجريان المماثلة بين العرضين، ولا لكونهما عرضين لجريانها بين الجوهرين، ولا لكونهما سوادين لجريانها في غيرهما، وكذا لا لكونهما بياضين أو طعمين أو رائحتين، ولا لكون المماثلة معنى قائما بالمتماثلين.. لجريانها بين العرضين مع استحالة قيام العرض بالعرض. المسألة وإذا امتنعت هذه الوجوه لم يبق إلا ما ذكر
The people have indeed differed concerning that. Al-Ash'ari and those who followed him claimed that the two resembling things (al-mushtabihān) and the two similars (al-mithlān) are two distinct things (ghayrān), each one of them taking the place of its companion. And the indication of restricting the definition to difference (al-mughāyara) is that a thing does not resemble nor be similar to itself, so it indicates that this (resemblance) runs between two different things. And the indication of their saying 'it takes its place' is that that which does not take the place of its companion is not considered a like (mithl) for it, even if there is agreement between them in many attributes, like blackness with whiteness, for they are not similars even though each of them is existent, accident, and color. So the absence of similitude between these two colors, along with the occurrence of agreement between them in the attributes we mentioned, indicates that there is no similitude with the establishment of difference in any respect, because when difference is established, one does not take the place of its companion. And this is the point: that between difference and similitude is a relation of opposition like that between two contraries, so it is inconceivable that they come together in one locus. So it follows necessarily from the establishment of difference that similitude is negated. And because the course of states (sīr al-aḥwāl) shows the correctness of this definition. For the two similars (al-mutamāthilān) are not similars because they are two substances (jawharayn), since similitude runs between accidents; nor because they are two accidents, since it runs between substances; nor because they are two blacknesses, since it runs in others; and likewise not because they are two whitenesses, two tastes, or two smells; nor because similitude is a meaning (ma'nā) subsisting in the two similars, since it runs between accidents while it is impossible for an accident to subsist in an accident. The issue: And when these ways are impossible, nothing remains except what was mentioned.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage defines similitude (tamāthul) from the Ash'ari perspective and provides a multi-part proof. It begins by stating the definition: two things are similars if each can take the place of the other (yasuddu kullu wāḥidin minhumā masadda ṣāḥibihi). Then it gives two indications (dalāla) supporting this definition. The first indication is that the definition ties similitude to difference (al-mughāyara) because a thing does not resemble itself, so resemblance only occurs between two distinct things. The second indication is that if something does not take the place of another, it is not considered a like, regardless of how many attributes they share. The example of black and white illustrates this: they share many general attributes (existence, accident, color) but are not similars because they differ in their specific attributes. The passage then explains the opposition between difference and similitude: they are like contraries and cannot coexist. It further refutes alternative explanations for why two things might be similars—such as being substances, accidents, or specific qualities—by showing that similitude runs across categories, not within them. Finally, it concludes that only the given definition remains valid.

مثلين
مخالفة
مغايرة
عرض
جوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Similitude requires numerical identity or that difference and similitude can coexist.
Correction: Similitude does not require identity; it requires mutual replaceability. Difference and similitude are contraries and cannot coexist.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari criterion for two things to be considered similars (mithlān)?
A) They share the most specific attribute
B) They share all attributes
C) Each can take the place of the other
D) They are in the same category

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari definition of similitude is mutual substitutability, which is opposed to difference.

What would it mean to take this seriously today?

How will you renounce that contradiction and live in the truth that nothing is like Him?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate

Names Do Not Imply Likeness in Meaning
Argues that shared names do not entail sameness in meaning, using examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why shared names between God and creation do not imply any likeness in meaning or attributes.

Core Idea
A common name does not create a common essence.

This passage presents a key principle in Islamic theology: naming alone does not establish similarity. The author uses concrete examples to show that names are arbitrary labels, and then applies this to the relationship between God and His creation. By grasping this argument, you will avoid a common misconception about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the examples of whiteness and opposition.
Notice the author begins by stating that proofs only manifest, not cause existence.
2
Understand the core claim
The key point: names refer to the realities of things but do not affect them. Existence and change are independent of the name applied.
3
Examine the examples
The author gives two examples: two white objects with different names remain similar; two opposite things with the same name remain different. See how names are arbitrary markers.
This shows naming does not change the nature of things.
4
Follow the dialectic
The author then applies the argument to the opponent: if they say there is likeness in meaning between God and creation, they contradict their rejection of shared names. If they deny likeness, then shared names imply no agreement.
This is a logical trap that reinforces the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على حقائق المسميات، وأحوالها، وأوصافها، والدليل لا أثر له في المدلول إلا بالإظهار، فأما الوجود أو التغير فلا يتعلق بالدليل ألا ترى أنا لو رأينا بياضين فسمينا أحدهما باسم والآخر باسم آخر لا تثبت بينهما مخالفة بمخالفة الاسم ولا تزول المماثلة الثابتة بمخالفة الاسم، ولو سمينا مختلفين أو متضادين باسم واحد لا يوجب ذلك التماثل بينهما وارتفاع المخالفة الثابتة بينهما ليصيرا متماثلين من جميع الوجوه بثبوت موافقة في الاسم بحيث لا مخالفة بينهما فيه، وإذا كان كذلك يقال لهم هل بين من تنسبون إليه ثبوت العالم وبين شيء من العالم مماثلة في المعنى؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، فقد وقعوا في شيء مما أبوا لأنهم دفعوا الاشتراك في الاسم لئلا يقع به التشبيه من حيث الاسم، وأثبتوا التشبيه في المعنى، وإن قالوا: لا. قلنا لهم ونحن نساعدكم على هذا فيعد ذلك بإطلاق اسم يطلق مثل ذلك الاسم على غيره لا يتغير عما كان عليه ولا تثبت به الموافقة.
Upon the realities of named things, their states, and their attributes; and the proof has no effect on what is proved except by manifestation. As for existence or change, it does not attach to the proof. Do you not see that if we saw two whitenesses and named one with a name and the other with another name, no dissimilarity is established between them by the difference of name, nor does the established similarity disappear by the difference of name. And if we named two different or opposite things with one name, that does not necessitate similarity between them and removal of the established dissimilarity between them so that they become identical in all respects by the establishment of agreement in the name such that there is no dissimilarity between them in it. And if this is so, it is said to them: Is there between the One to Whom you attribute the existence of the world and any thing of the world a likeness in meaning? If they say: Yes, then they have fallen into something they rejected, because they rejected sharing in the name lest resemblance occur by way of the name, and they affirmed resemblance in meaning. And if they say: No, we say to them: And we agree with you on this, and then the application of a name like that name to another does not change it from what it was, nor does agreement become established by it.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument that shared names do not imply sameness in meaning. It begins by stating that names refer to the realities, states, and attributes of things, but the proof (the name) has no effect on the thing itself except to manifest it. Existence and change are not caused by the name. The author gives an example: two white objects named differently remain similar; two different things named the same remain different. Thus names are arbitrary and do not affect the underlying reality. The author then applies this to the question of God and the world: the opponent is asked whether there is a likeness in meaning between the creator of the world and any part of the world. If they say yes, they contradict themselves because they had rejected a shared name that might imply resemblance, but now they admit resemblance in meaning. If they say no, then applying the same name (e.g., 'existent') to both God and creatures does not create any agreement in meaning. This establishes the principle that divine attributes, though expressed with the same words as human attributes, do not imply any likeness.

Key Terms
حقائق المسميات
المدلول
الإظهار
الاشتراك في الاسم
المماثلة في المعنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Shared names between God and creation imply similarity in essence or attributes.
Correction: The passage shows that names are arbitrary and do not affect reality; therefore, shared names do not create similarity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if two different things are given the same name?
A) They become similar in all respects.
B) Their dissimilarity is removed.
C) It does not necessitate similarity between them.
D) They become identical in meaning.

Key Takeaway
Shared names between God and creatures do not imply any similarity in essence or attributes.

The Transcendental Negation Formula and the Maturidi Position
Introduces the formula 'not like...' to negate resemblance and distinguishes Maturidi from Ash'ari view on likeness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi method of adding explicit negations like 'not like other existents' to divine attributes to safeguard against false imaginings, and how this differs from the Ash'ari definition of likeness as equality in all respects.

Core Idea
Adding 'not like' safeguards divine transcendence without denying the attribute.

In the previous lesson, we learned that shared names do not entail likeness. The Maturidis go further by attaching explicit negations like 'not like other existents' to protect the unlearned from false imaginings. This passage explains the rationale, credits Abu Hanifa as the pioneer, and distinguishes this approach from the Ash'ari definition of likeness as equality in all respects.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic and translation once through to get the overall flow of the author's argument.
The passage ends abruptly with 'بل' due to the page break.
2
Notice the Formula
Identify the repeated pattern 'not like...' in the examples: existent not like existents, living not like living beings, knowing not like knowers. Reflect on why this formula is used.
The formula affirms the attribute while negating any resemblance to creation.
3
Recognize Abu Hanifa's Role
The passage credits Abu Hanifa as the first to give this advice to the common people. Note that later theologians followed his method.
This shows the Maturidi school's reliance on the founder's approach.
4
Contrast with Ash'ari View
Understand the distinction: Ash'aris define likeness (mumathala) as equality in all respects, so any difference negates likeness. Maturidis do not require total equality to negate likeness.
The passage is cut off, but the contrast is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم مع هذا أنا نلحق بالاسم المشترك ما يوجب نفي ما يسبق إلى الوهم من معنى المماثلة، وإن لم يكن اللفظ متعرضا لذلك صيانة منا لأوهام من لا دراية له بالحقائق عن لحوق وصمة وعيب. فنقول: هو موجود لا كالموجودين، حي لا كالأحياء، عالم لا كالعلماء، وكذا في جميع الصفات ليخبر به عن ثبوت ما هو ثابت من حقيقة المعنى، ونفي ما هو منفي عن المماثلة، وأبو حنيفة – رحمه الله هو السابق إلى هذه النصيحة لعامة الخلق ثم اتبعه المتكلمون على ذلك. ثم اعلم بأنا لا نقول ما يقول الأشعرية: إن لا مماثلة إلا بالمساواة من جميع الوجوه، وأن لا ثبوت لها مع تمكن المخالفة من وجه لتنافيها من وجه، بل ...
Then, despite this, we attach to the shared name that which necessitates negation of what leaps to the mind of the meaning of likeness, even if the word itself does not imply that, as a safeguard from us for the fancies of those who have no knowledge of realities, lest they incur a stain and defect. So we say: He is existent not like other existents, living not like other living beings, knowing not like other knowers, and likewise in all attributes, so that it informs about the affirmation of what is affirmed of the reality of the meaning, and the negation of what is negated of likeness. And Abu Hanifa – may Allah have mercy on him – was the first to give this advice to the common people, then the theologians followed him in that. Then know that we do not say what the Ash'aris say: 'There is no likeness except by equality in all respects, and that it is not established when difference is possible from one aspect because they contradict from that aspect,' but...

Commentary
This passage explains the Maturidi method of safeguarding divine transcendence. Even though shared names do not entail likeness in meaning, the Maturidis still add explicit negations like 'not like other existents' to prevent false imaginings from those ignorant of realities. This formula affirms the real meaning of the attribute while negating any likeness. The author credits Abu Hanifa as the pioneer of this precautionary teaching, followed by later mutakallimun. Then a distinction is drawn from the Ash'ari position: the Ash'aris define likeness (mumathala) as equality in all respects, and thus they say that if there is even one difference, likeness is negated. The Maturidis do not define it that way; for them, likeness can be negated even if there is similarity in some aspects, as long as the reality differs. The passage ends incomplete, but the contrast is clear.

The Exhaustive Division Argument Against Anthropomorphism
Presents an exhaustive division argument against anthropomorphism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument used by the mutakallimun to refute anthropomorphism (tashbih) by showing that any similarity between God and creation leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
If God were like creation in any way, absurdity follows — so He must be absolutely unique.

The mutakallimun developed a powerful logical argument against those who claimed God resembles created beings. This argument relies on an exhaustive division: either God is like the world in all respects or in some. Both options lead to contradiction because the world is temporal and God is eternal. This passage presents that argument and connects it to the Ash'ari tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation to grasp the full argument.
Notice the logical structure: if-then-else.
2
Identify the two cases of the division
Find the two cases: God being like the world in all respects, or in some respect. The argument uses an exhaustive division (either all or some).
The term 'وجه' means 'respect' or 'aspect'.
3
Follow the reasoning for each case
For each case, the argument derives a contradiction: if all respects, then either the eternal becomes temporal or the temporal becomes eternal. If some respect, then in that respect the same contradiction applies.
The contradiction forces the rejection of the original assumption of similarity.
4
Note the conclusion and historical note
The conclusion is that God cannot be like the world in any way. The passage also notes that al-Ash'ari used this argument, inherited from earlier mutakallimun.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحقيقه أن أئمة أهل الكلام استدلوا على المشبهة في نفي التشبيه فقالوا: لو كان الله مثلا للعالم إما أن كان مثلا له من جميع الوجوه أو كان مثلا له من وجه دون وجه، فلو كان مثلا له من جميع الوجوه لكان القديم محدثا من جميع الوجوه، أو كان العالم قديما من جميع الوجوه، لأن العالم بجميع وجوهه محدث، والباري جل وعلا قديم بجميع صفاته، وإن كان مثلا له من وجه لكان محدثا من ذلك الوجه أو كان العالم قديما من ذلك الوجه لأن العالم من ذلك الوجه محدث والباري قديم. والأشعري احتج على المشبهة في كتبه على هذا الوجه على ما توارثه ممن سبقه من متكلمى أهل السنة وهذا يبين لك صحة ما ادعيت أن المماثلة بجهة المخالفة من من جهة أخرى حيث قسموا الكلام على هذا التقسيم، غير أن مع تثبت المماثلة بجهة تكون بعد استوائهما في تلك الجهة بدليل أنهم أثبتوا حدوثه، أو قدم العالم بتلك الجهة، وبالوقوف على هذا يعرف صحة ما ادعيت وثبوته عند المتكلمين
Its verification: The imams of the people of kalam argued against the Mushabbiha (anthropomorphists) in negating tashbih (anthropomorphism) and said: If God were a like (مثل) of the world, either He is like it in all respects or He is like it in some respect without others. If He were like it in all respects, then the eternal would be temporally originated in all respects, or the world would be eternal in all respects, because the world in all its respects is temporally originated, and the Creator, exalted and high, is eternal in all His attributes. And if He were like it in some respect, then He would be temporally originated in that respect, or the world would be eternal in that respect, because the world in that respect is temporally originated and the Creator is eternal. And al-Ash'ari argued against the Mushabbiha in his books in this way, according to what he inherited from those before him of the mutakallimun of Ahl al-Sunna. And this shows you the correctness of what I claimed, that مماثلة is established in an aspect of difference from another aspect, for they divided the discourse in this division. However, مماثلة is established in an aspect after their equality in that aspect, as evidenced by their establishing its temporal origination or the eternity of the world in that aspect. And by understanding this, the correctness of my claim and its establishment among the mutakallimun is known.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage presents the classic kalam argument against the Mushabbiha (those who ascribe anthropomorphic similarity to God). The argument uses an exhaustive division: if God is like the world, either in all respects or in some. Both lead to contradiction: all aspects would make the eternal temporal or the temporal eternal; some aspects would still force temporal origination or eternity in that aspect. The author notes that al-Ash'ari used this same argument. The passage also reinforces the earlier point that مماثلة requires equality in the relevant aspect, as the argument hinges on the aspect under consideration.
